Global Finance: Africa Central Banks Face Rate Dilemma
Global Finance: Africa Central Banks Face Rate Dilemma
By Matina Stevis and Patrick McGroarty
815 words
15 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
As the Federal Reserve prepares to raise interest rates, Africa is feeling the pinch.
The continent's central bankers ought to be cutting rates to offset the drag from the commodity-price collapse
and China's slowing economic growth. Instead, monetary-policy officials from Angola to Zambia are raising
them to shore up their currencies, fight inflation and stanch capital flight, as the prospect of higher U.S. rates
makes the dollar more attractive.
The circumstances leave African central bankers with few attractive options and fixated on Washington even
as economic growth across sub-Saharan Africa crawls to its slowest pace in a decade.
"Any action that you take in this sort of situation has costs," said John Ashbourne, Africa economist for
consultancy Capital Economics. "I don't envy the choice."
In South Africa, the continent's most globally integrated economy, low gold and iron-ore prices and political
problems have dragged growth down to 1.4% this year, with a forecast for even slower growth next year. Still,
the central bank raised its main interest rate last month by 0.25 percentage point to 6.25%. Reserve Bank
Gov. Lesetja Kganyago said that if he didn't raise rates the Fed's looming increase could spark steep capital
outflows.
"Will the move by the Fed affect us? Of course it will," he said.
Four rate increases over the past two years have so far failed to stop the rand from shedding more than half
of its value against the dollar. The rand weakened to record lows beyond 16 to the dollar on Friday, after
President Jacob Zuma fired his respected finance minister with no explanation and named a widely
denounced appointee to take his place.
The rand rebounded 4.7% Monday as Mr. Zuma gave in to pressure, dismissing the new appointee and
bringing back Pravin Gordhan, who had held the role of finance minister from 2009 to 2014.
Elsewhere in Africa, landlocked and import-dependent Uganda has lifted its main rate five times this year.
Zambia, a major Chinese copper supplier where thousands of miners have lost their jobs and the local
currency has collapsed, last month raised its benchmark rate by three percentage points to 15.5%.
Mozambique in October raised its rates for the first time since 2011.
"Our concern is to maintain stability," said Guadalberto Limo Campos, a deputy governor at the National
Bank of Angola, which raised its benchmark rate a quarter percentage point in August amid a domestic
economic crisisthat is pushing annual inflation up to nearly 14%. Growth, meanwhile, is crashing to around
3.5% annually, from double-digit rates a few years ago.
Angola and Mozambique also have made it harder to move money abroad or buy dollars, hoping to stem the
exodus of foreign currency. Nigeria has imposed similar restrictions and frozen its exchange rate at around
200 naira to the dollar.
Nigeria, the continent's top economy and crude producer, is one of the few to brave rate cuts to spur growth
that has slowed to its slowest pace in a decade. Last month, Nigeria's central bank unexpectedly cut its main
rate by two percentage points to 11%.
But most African countries see higher rates as the only way to slow foreign money from departing after U.S.
interest rates rise.
In Kenya, East Africa's biggest economy, the central bank has lifted rates twice this year by a total of three
percentage points to 11.5% and has spent $1 billion in currency reserves to control inflation and buttress the

--- Page 6 ---
Page 6 of 210 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
shilling. Kenya doesn't depend on mineral or oil exports, meaning it is better placed to deal with the
turbulence hitting many other African economies.
Patrick Njoroge, Kenya's central-bank governor, said he was worried his country would be lumped in with
others like South Africa by fretful investors after the Fed raises rates.
"They will flock in and flock out, herd behavior. . . . We've really been fighting to distinguish ourselves from
others," he said in an interview earlier this month.
The International Monetary Fund has stepped in to help Kenya and other countries, including with a $700
million fund that Nairobi can draw upon if its dollar reserves drop too low. Officials haven't used it yet.
The IMF extended similar assistance to Mozambique last month and Ghana earlier this year. Zambia last
month began talks about its own IMF support package, but a deal hasn't been reached.
"By and large what you want is the exchange rate to move, to allow as much depreciation as possible; you
don't want to wither away your reserves,"saidAbebe Aemro Selassie, deputy Africa chief for the IMF,
explaining the conundrum facing central bankers across Africa today. "But you don't want to have disorderly
market conditions, either."

The Intelligent Investor: Learning From the Oil Bust
The Intelligent Investor: Learning From the Oil Bust
By Jason Zweig
803 words
12 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The implosion in energy prices has devastated many investors' portfolios. If only it would also devastate old
ways of thinking.
As oil sank below $36 this past week, the share prices of dozens of energy limited partnerships were down at
least 50% for the year.
Many income-craving investors plunged into energy stocks for their high yield. But oil-and-gas companies
have fallen so far, so fast, that more than four years' worth of income have been wiped out by the recent
capital losses.
But chasing yield beyond the safety of government bonds is a sure way to lose a chunk of your principal. You
might never learn that until you can let go of the delusion that the future will repeat the past.
Just look at the torrent of money that poured into energy stocks.
Between year-end 2007 and the end of 2014, oil-and-gas companies raised $255.7 billion in initial public
offerings, subsequent share offerings and convertible-bond issues, according to Ipreo, a financial-research
firm.
Over the same period, asset managers launched almost 100 mutual funds, exchange-traded funds and
closed-end funds specializing in energy stocks. The investing public pumped $64 billion into these funds.
But almost no one foresaw oil below $40 (including me) -- just as almost no one imagined that real-estate
prices could drop before the 2008-2009 financial crisis.
As oil approached its peak above $140 in mid-2008, Goldman Sachs predicted that black gold would hit $150
to $200 a barrel, and other Wall Street firms weren't far behind.
The analysts' view of the future was consistently anchored on the present.
After oil shot up to $140 in June 2008, analysts cranked up their estimates of its year-end price 5% to nearly
$113. Then, when the oil price fell by more than half between September 2008 and January 2009, the
analysts halved their predictions for the year-end 2009 price. Future expectations have moved almost in lock
step with current price ever since.
By extrapolating the recent past, oil analysts were only human.
Looking at nearly 38,000 forecasts of stock prices and other assets, behavioral economist Werner De Bondt
found that investors incorrigibly project the recent past into the future. In one of his surveys, every 1% rise in
the Dow over the previous week made people 1.3% more likely to be bullish on stocks over the coming six
months.
Other researchers have found similar effects among investment-newsletter editors and stock analysts. The
investing mind not only sees what it wants to see; it bases that on what it is already seeing.
If oil stocks have burned you, use that as motivation to rethink how you form your expectations of the future.
That requires breaking from the past and your peers.
Charlie Munger, vice chairman at Warren Buffett's Berkshire Hathaway, has long urged investors to "invert,
always invert." By considering the opposite of the conventional expectation, you can break the habit of
chasing past performance.
Mr. Munger did that in March 2009, when he sank millions of dollars into Wells Fargo & Co. With many other
investors fearing that the financial system would collapse, he asked: How will this bank do if it survives?

--- Page 8 ---
Page 8 of 210 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Virtually everyone expects the Federal Reserve to raise short-term interest rates next week. That doesn't
guarantee that all the experts are wrong. But it does mean you should ask yourself whether you are prepared
for the Fed to do the unexpected and leave rates unchanged. (Hint: Stocks would probably stumble, and
bonds would likely get a lift.)
Likewise, almost no one expects inflation to flare up again. And it probably won't -- but that doesn't mean it
can't.
So ask yourself or your financial adviser: What is the probability that inflation will exceed an annual rate of
2% in the next 12 months? What events could lead to that scenario?
Unless you think the odds of 2% inflation are zero, you should own some Treasuryinflation-protected
securities or a TIPS fund that can benefit when their prices are adjusted upward when inflation takes an
unexpected jump.
In short, you can't get higher income just because you want or need to. There isn't a safe way to make, say,
6% in a world of 2% bonds.
The best way to raise your yield isn't to buy what is popular, but to wait until other investors are extrapolating
misery -- and then buy.
Mr. Munger likes to point out that successful investing requires "this crazy combination of gumption and
patience, and then being ready to pounce when the opportunity presents itself."
Energy stocks, high-yield bonds and emerging markets might be nearing those levels soon.

BofA Passes Its Stress Test --- Federal Reserve gives the lender clearance but says it has work to do before the next exam
BofA Passes Its Stress Test --- Federal Reserve gives the lender clearance but says it has work to do
before the next exam
By Christina Rexrode
689 words
11 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Federal Reserve on Thursday gave Bank of America Corp . a passing grade on its resubmitted "stress
test" but stopped short of granting the firm a gold star.
While saying the bank needed to "continue to make steady, demonstrable progress" in its capital planning,
the Fed gave Bank of America the nod to keep paying its dividend and buying back shares under its $4 billion
repurchase plan. Those moves are important to investors, who have been frustrated by the bank's relatively
low dividends and share price.
The Fed's rebuke in March, when the central bank said it had concerns about the way the lender modeled
for losses and revenue, was a black eye for Bank of America . It meant that the lender has had more
stress-test miscues than any of its peers, and raised questions about the firm's ability to discern what
regulators want.
Bank of America spent $100 million to prepare for the resubmission, including hiring consultants and some
new employees.
The Fed said Thursday that the bank "has made progress" in fixing its capital-planning deficiencies. But it
also said the bank must continue to improve before the next stress-test submissions in April. The Fed didn't
issue the same exhortation to J.P. Morgan Chase & Co . and Goldman Sachs Group Inc . when they went
through a similar resubmission process in 2013.
Bank of America noted the Fed's approval in a statement, but didn't provide further comment. The lender's
shares rose 10 cents, or 0.6%, to $17.20.
The bank's next stress test will be run by Andrea Smith , a longtime human-resources executive who was
elevated to chief administrative officer by Chairman and Chief Executive Brian Moynihan this summer. Terry
Laughlin, who ran this year's resubmission and has extensive regulatory experience, will move to running the
wealth-management division. The bank says that Mr. Laughlin will help with the transition.
The bank's capital levels in this year's test, which measures how banks would fare in a severe recession,
were above regulators' requirements. But the Fed had raised concerns about the quality of the bank's
process, including the level of detail in the risk models the bank was using, according to people familiar with
the situation.
For example, with some loans the bank used the same models to calculate potential risks and losses for both
domestic and international loans, according to these people. In other cases, the bank relied too heavily on
intuition rather than computer models when making loss predictions, some of these people said.
The Fed also had voiced worry that the bank's internal auditors weren't sufficiently overseeing or reviewing
the main stress-test process, according to these people.
Regulators also had concerns about a decentralized stress-test process, with various units in the bank
calculating their own potential losses with their own methodology, according to some of these people.
Regulators also told top bank executives they weren't "forward looking," and should have anticipated
problems with their stress-test submissions, The Wall Street Journal previously reported.
Mr. Moynihan has acknowledged that the bank's stress-test processes had "plateaued."
The bank has worked on making sure all its business lines are regularly involved in assessing how their
actions might affect the stress test, rather than viewing it as a once-a-year exercise, and trying to bring their
own risk models in line with what they believe the Fed measures.

--- Page 10 ---
Page 10 of 210 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
At a Wednesday conference, Mr. Moynihan said the lender had also worked on "getting the data flows nailed
down," which can be a challenge in a bank stitched together from multiple acquisitions.
"For our commercial real estate, we have to go back and get data from previous companies' files that we
wouldn't have kept because the loans kind of were out of the system," Mr. Moynihan said.
He also said BofA "wanted to be more careful to make sure we really nail this thing."

China Adds to Its Piles of Gold
Commodities
China Adds to Its Piles of Gold
By Biman Mukherji
722 words
11 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
HONG KONG -- While gold has crashed to multiyear lows as the strengthening U.S. dollar spooks investors,
the bearish sentiment hasn't daunted one quiet operator: China's central bank.
After six years of disclosing nothing about its gold reserves, the People's Bank of China surprised markets in
July by announcing that its holdings of the metal had risen by more than 50% since 2009to 1,658 metric tons.
The central bank has been steadily scooping up more gold since then, with its reserves climbing another
5.1% to 1,743 metric tons at the end of November.
Some believe that without such central-bank support, gold might already have fallen much further. Gold
futures traded at $1,071.60 an ounce Thursday morning on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile
Exchange, hovering around the lowest level in nearly six years. They closed down $4.50, or 0.4%, at
$1,073.10.
"Had it not been for China and Russia's central banks buying cheap gold, the price would have gone below
$1,000 dollars an ounce," said Bill Hubard, chief economist at Bullion Capital, a global physical bullion
exchange.
The PBOC didn't respond to a request for comment on its gold buying.
Analysts say the central bank likely has a few motives, including a desire to plow its vast foreign-exchange
reserves into a broader range of assets and to follow other leading global central banks in building up gold
reserves as a store of value.
The bank's gold holdings currently are equivalent to only 1.6% of its foreign-exchange reserves, much lower
than those held by Western central banks, many of which keep more than 60% of their reserves in gold,
according to Ross Norman, chief executive of London-based gold brokerage Sharps Pixley Ltd.
China's gold reserves are about 20% of those held by the U.S. Federal Reserve.
"We would not be surprised to see purchases by China's central bank for many years to come as they are
underweight in gold for an economy of its size," Mr. Norman said, adding he expected the PBOC to keep
buying gold when prices dipped.
China's move to buy more gold this year may have been driven in part by its successful push to have its
currency, the yuan, accepted as a global reserve currency by the International Monetary Fund. With more
gold in the central bank's coffers, investors could have more confidence in holding the yuan, analysts said.
"We believe that Chinese accumulation of gold reserves will continue as a means of diversification of reserve
holdings as well as a signal of strength," said Seamus Donoghue, chief executive of Allocated Bullion
Solutions, a Singapore-based international bullion-trading network.
The PBOC isn't the only central bank that has been stocking up on gold. Russia expanded its gold reserves
by 77.2 tons in the third quarter, according to the World Gold Council, bringing its holdings to 1,352 metric
tons. Like the Chinese, the Russian central bank is rapidly divesting U.S. dollar assets, said Mr. Norman of
Sharps Pixley. "The Russian rate of accumulation in 2015 is steepening, and we see plenty of scope for this
to be maintained," he said.
Other central banks boosting gold holdings include those in the United Arab Emirates, Ukraine, Malaysia,
Kazakhstan, Jordan and Belarus.
Despite the central-bank buying, gold prices primarily are determined by traders buying and selling on
electronic platforms, as well as by consumer demand for jewelry, particularly in India and China.

--- Page 12 ---
Page 12 of 210 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Analysts believe prices could yet fall below $1,000 an ounce if the Fed moves ahead with an expected
increase in interest rates next week. A rate increase would likely strengthen the dollar further, and assets
priced in U.S. dollars, such as gold, tend to fall when the dollar rises as they become more expensive for
non-U.S. buyers.
"If the Fed rate hike comes to pass, gold should touch $1,050 per ounce," said Barnabas Chen, an analyst at
OCBC Bank. He said gold could drop even further to $950 an ounce by the end of next year if the Fed
continues with rate increases.
---
Yifan Xie in Shanghai contributed to this article.

Rebound in Energy Arena Lifts Stocks --- Major indexes snap three-session slump driven by falling commodities prices
Thursday's Markets
Rebound in Energy Arena Lifts Stocks --- Major indexes snap three-session slump driven by falling
commodities prices
By Leslie Josephs and Christopher Whittall
613 words
11 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks rose as investors snatched up beaten-down energy-company shares.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 82.45 points, or 0.5%, to 17574.75. Chevron, the index's biggest
gainer, rose $1.70, or 1.9%, to $89.30.
The S&P 500 rose 4.61, or 0.2%, to 2052.23. Its energy-company index gained 0.6% but still is down 8% so
far in December. Year to date, the S&P 500 is down 0.3%.
"No one's going to make their year in the last three weeks," said Kenny Polcari, director at O'Neil Securities.
Shares of several miners and energy companies have dropped this week as commodities prices fell.
Crude-oil prices fell again Thursday after the Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries said it
pumped more oil last month than any month in three years. U.S. crude dropped 1.1% to $36.76 a barrel, the
lowest since 2009.
"If oil prices stay here or go down, there will be severe consequences" for energy companies, said Henrik
Drusebjerg, chief strategist at Carnegie Investment Bank, noting that some companies won't survive and
energy-dependent economies could struggle.
In corporate news, major oil company ConocoPhillips estimated capital expenditures of $7.7 billion for next
year, a 25% decline from the reduced levels the company expects to spend for 2015. Its shares rose 75
cents, or 1.6%, to 49.22.
Steve Ells, co-chief executive and founder of Chipotle Mexican Grill, apologized on national television for the
company's food-safety crises and detailed how the company was inspecting its food-safety procedures. Its
shares rose 27.42, or 5%, to 575.43.
Meanwhile, investors were looking ahead to the Dec. 15-16 Federal Reserve meeting, when the U.S. central
bank is widely expected to raise interest rates for the first time in almost a decade.
Ultralow interest rates have boosted stock markets in recent years. But many analysts and investors say a
rate increase won't put an end to the bull run, as it would be a vote of confidence in the economy.
"I think the U.S. economy is healthy enough to withstand it," said Frances Hudson, a strategist at Standard
Life Investments, which oversees GBP 250 billion ($379.5 billion) in assets.
While the U.S. central bank is backing away from its easy-money policies, central banks in Europe and
Japan remain accommodative.
Last week, the European Central Bank pushed a key interest rate further into negative territory and extended
the duration of its bond-buying program to try to boost sagging inflation.
Investors also expect the Bank of Japan to maintain its easy-money policies for some time.
"You'll see people favoring European equities and Japanese equities over the U.S. and U.K.," Ms. Hudson
said.
The Stoxx Europe 600 fell 0.3% Thursday, its third consecutive day of declines.
In Asia on Thursday, Japan's Nikkei Stock Average dropped 1.3%, Australia's S&P/ASX 200 declined 0.8%
and China's Shanghai Composite Index fell 0.5%.

The euro remains up 3.6% against the dollar since the start of December, rising after the ECB underwhelmed

Style & Travel -- On Style: The Warm (and Stylish) Technology in Your Jacket --- Choices abound this winter as PrimaLoft and Polartec help...
Style & Travel -- On Style: The Warm (and Stylish) Technology in Your Jacket --- Choices abound this
winter as PrimaLoft and Polartec help drive changes; innovation in insulation
By Christina Binkley
1,024 words
10 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Staying warm and dry outside has never been so complicated.
New and improved insulating materials are creating a wealth of new options in coats. Wool, down and
polyester are being treated and blended in new ways as manufacturers seek to make the perfect coat -- one
that works as well at a mall as it does on a mountain. But sorting out the cruelty-free down from the washable
wool, "hydrophobic" synthetics and various blends can make buying an insulated coat a full-on research
project.
The new textile technology is being driven in part by changes in people's outdoors activities. Apparel
manufacturers are finding that people spend less time out of doors, but work more intensely when they are
outside. Busy lives leave less time for leisurely all-day hikes. Yet plenty of weekend warriors run, snowshoe
or participate in other sweat-inducing sports. They get in and out of heated cars. All this demands versatile
outerwear that provides warmth but also releases moisture and breathes.
Outdoor enthusiasts are demanding better fashion as well -- slimmer fits especially -- as the lines blur
between athletic wear and daily apparel. Apparel makers are keenly aware that their Instagram and
Facebook-loving consumers want to be forever photo-ready.
"The new outdoor athletes in their 20s just won't wear it if they think it doesn't look good," says Greg
Thomsen, U.S. managing director of Adidas Outdoor, a unit of the global athletic-wear giant.
For decades, the choice for a parka's insulation was between down and synthetic materials. Down, made of
goose or duck plumage, was luxurious, lightweight and packable, but it was expensive and lost its ability to
insulate when wet. Synthetics -- made of polyester fibers -- were bulky and heavy, but warmed even when
damp.
Some of the latest innovations are being driven by two big U.S. apparel-insulation manufacturers, PrimaLoft
and Polartec. The U.S. military inspired some recent improvements in synthetics a few years ago by asking
U.S. manufacturers to develop warm, dry and lightweight alternatives to down for soldiers working in
mountainous terrain.
"Special forces can't just layer up and layer down when people start shooting at them," says Doug Kelliher,
vice president of product management at Polartec, which developed a polyester insulation for the military and
then introduced it to consumers three years ago, branding it "Alpha." Like fuzzy lace, the fibers are knitted
together and laid on a mesh material that doesn't bunch, so coats don't need to be quilted or baffled (with
extra pockets of down sewn in).
Outdoor Research won a "2016 Gear of the Year" award from Outside Magazine for its $299 UberLayer
jacket, insulated with the Polartec Alpha material. The award noted the jacket's ability to move moisture away
from the body during heavy activity.
Mountain Khakis, a brand that focuses on highly functional everyday clothes, is using the "hydrophobic"
Alpha insulation in outdoor workwear. "It dries 60% faster than other synthetics, and it's more breathable,"
says Noah Robertson, Mountain Khakis' co-founder and director of product development and design.
Concerns about sustainability and animal cruelty are also fueling innovations. A pioneer in environmentally
friendly production, Patagonia pledges that its down is cruelty-free and traceable -- meaning it can be traced
back to birds that were never force-fed or live-plucked.
PrimaLoft -- used by outdoor apparel makers from Marmot to Adidas -- has also initiated a "traceable down"
program, hiring third-party auditors to inspect its producers, who are mainly in China.

--- Page 16 ---
Page 16 of 210 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"We can trace our down all the way to the egg," says Mike Joyce, PrimaLoft's president and chief executive.
PrimaLoft is shifting its production of synthetic fibers over to recycled polyester. (Currently about 20% of its
synthetics are recycled.) The company is blending natural materials like down and wool with synthetics, and it
is treating down with a water-repellent substance. PrimaLoft is also testing wool-blend insulation, which it
expects to see on apparel in stores by 2017.
PrimaLoft's down-synthetic blends, launched in 2013, are being used by outdoor brands including Helly
Hansen and Black Diamond, whose Hot Forge Hoody, with PrimaLoft's "Gold" down-synthetic blend
insulation, won a 2015 "Editors Choice" award from Backcountry Magazine.
New treatments are even making old-fashioned wool more versatile. Smartwool -- known for its washable
wool T-shirts, socks and other apparel -- is using wool as an insulating material in jackets designed for
running and other winter exercise that generates body heat. For its "SmartLoft" insulation, wool fibers are
rolled, creating an effect like split ends on hair, says Eric Henderson, a Smartwool spokesman.
Based in Colorado, he says he went skinning -- a form of cross-country skiing -- in one of the jackets last
week. "I got home, it was stinky, I just threw it in the [washing] machine."
In my own trials of about a dozen styles of coats, the down-synthetic blend failed to match the sheer light
packable pleasure of 100% down, yet it didn't seem that much lighter than the full-on synthetics. (Mr. Joyce
says that may be because of heavy outer fabrics.)
The pleasures of down are many -- not the least of which is the price these days. A glut of down has prices
lower than in many years, and that is reducing the cost of down coats to bring them on par with synthetics.
Smartwool's lay-flat technology and stretch materials created some of the most stylish designs I saw. For
wicking and fast-dry qualities -- issues of survival in the wilderness, if not the mall -- the new synthetics are
hard to beat.
David Newey, director of global marketing for PrimaLoft, points to North Face's ThermoBall jackets, which
use PrimaLoft insulation that the company says is as compressible as down and is ultra-fast-drying.
"You could fall into a lake, and it would dry while you hike back to safety," Mr. Newey says.

Page 17 of 210 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Energy Rout Sinks Stocks --- More declines in energy and metals prices hit shares of corresponding firms
Tuesday's Markets
Energy Rout Sinks Stocks --- More declines in energy and metals prices hit shares of corresponding
firms
By Saumya Vaishampayan and Riva Gold
668 words
9 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks fell around the globe as investors sold shares of energy and mining companies amid a deepening
commodities rout.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average lost 162.51 points, or 0.9%, to 17568.00. The S&P lost 13.48 points, or
0.6%, to 2063.59. The Nasdaq Composite slipped 3.57 points, or 0.1%, to 5098.24.
Worries about oversupply and waning demand for a range of commodities have sent prices sharply lower,
with benchmark iron ore prices dropping to fresh decade lows and oil trading at a near seven-year trough.
"You see commodities hitting fresh lows every day," said Larry Weiss, head of trading at brokerage firm
Instinet. Oil prices "seem to be driving the broader tape," he added.
Stock swings have picked up in recent sessions.
On Thursday, the Dow industrials shed 1.4% after the European Central Bank's expanded stimulus efforts
disappointed investors. A day later, the blue-chip index rose 2.1% after a strong U.S. jobs report paved the
way for the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates. Volatility in oil prices also has fueled large stock moves.
Mining and energy stocks were among the major decliners Tuesday. Freeport-McMoRan, the U.S.'s biggest
miner, fell 49 cents, or 6.8%, to $6.74. Kinder Morgan lost 70 cents, or 4.3%, to 15.72. The energy company
said Tuesday that it is cutting its quarterly dividend payment by 75%.
Brent crude, the global oil benchmark, dipped below $40 a barrel for the first time since 2009 before
recovering to end at $40.26, down 1.2% for the day. U.S. crude oil fell 0.4% to $37.51 a barrel.
"Are we going to hit the bottom [for commodity prices and mining shares] or is this a bottomless pit? There's
just this relentless disconnect between supply and demand," said Jeremy Batstone-Carr, chief economist and
strategist at London-based brokerage and wealth manager Charles Stanley.
The S&P 500's materials sector, which includes miners, fell the most with a 1.9% loss.
The index's industrial stocks, which includes Southwest Airlines, lost 1.6%. Shares of the airline fell 4.47, or
9%, to 45.04 after the firm said a key revenue metric could fall in the current quarter.
Energy stocks in the S&P slipped 1.5%, pushing its loss so far this month to 9.7%.
The Shanghai Composite Index slid 1.9%, Japan's Nikkei Stock Average fell 1% and Australia's mining-heavy
S&P/ASX 200 lost 0.9%.
The Stoxx Europe 600 declined 1.8% as some of Europe's biggest miners fell sharply. Glencore declined
6.9%, Anglo American slumped 12%, and Rio Tinto dropped 8.4%. Anglo on Tuesday cut 85,000 jobs and
suspended its dividend payments, part of its plan to weather weak commodity markets.
Anglo's dividend suspension drove much of its stock decline on Tuesday, said Rebecca O'Keeffe, head of
investment at U.K. brokerage Interactive Investor.
"In a low yield environment, an awful lot of income investors are highly reliant on dividend payments, so you
tend to get a much more adverse reaction when companies actually elect to suspend their dividends," she
said.
Investors have flocked to stocks that pay dividends in recent years as central banks have held interest rates
low.

--- Page 18 ---
Page 18 of 210 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Earlier this year we were going for absolute yield in dividends, and now we're going for dividend growth,"
said John Augustine, chief investment officer of Columbus, Ohio-based Huntington Bank.
Some investors are waiting for the coming Fed announcement, due next week, before placing major bets,
traders said. The Fed hasn't raised short-term rates since 2006.
In other markets, the 10-year Treasury yield rose slightly to 2.239%, compared with 2.222% on Monday.
Yields rise as prices fall.

Stocks Drop as Oil Tumbles 5.8%

A selloff in energy shares dragged the broader stock market lower, as oil prices tumbled to an almost

"You've seen a lot of people over the last three or four months looking for a low in energy, but it just remains
elusive," said Michael Antonelli, equity sales trader at Robert W. Baird.
Crude-oil futures in New York fell $2.32, or 5.8%, to $37.65 a barrel as fears of a global supply glut deepened
after a meeting of the Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries ended last week with no agreement
to cut production.

fell $2.43, or 2.7%, to $87.28. Exxon declined 2.06, or 2.6%, to 76.80.

decade later this month. On Friday, stocks rose sharply after a stronger-than-expected U.S. jobs report firmed
expectations that the Fed will start raising rates at its Dec. 15-16 meeting.
"Friday's rally seemed like just a response to the jobs number and not a long-term breakout," said Tom
Carter, managing director at JonesTrading.
The swings have left the S&P 500 with a loss of 0.2% so far in December and a gain of 0.9% so far this year.
Ultralow interest rates have boosted equity markets in recent years. Many investors say they have been
assured by comments from Fed Chairwoman Janet Yellen suggesting monetary policy in the U.S. will
tighten at a gradual pace.
"The Fed is still pretty dovish," said Sanjiv Shah, chief investment officer at Sun Global Investments. The jobs

office-supply chain from buying rival Office Depot. The shares of Office Depot dropped 1.04, or 14%, to 5.59.

Venezuelans' Chance to Cast Off Chavismo
Americas
Venezuelans' Chance to Cast Off Chavismo
By Mary Anastasia O'Grady
823 words
7 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Venezuela held elections on Sunday for all 167 seats in its national assembly. The unicameral legislature,
which is renewed every five years, has been in the control of Hugo Chavez's Bolivarian Revolution for more
than 15 years. As we went to press official results had not been announced but this time opposition parties
were confident that they had sufficient popular support to wrest majority control from the chavistas.
Yet Venezuela hasn't held a fair, transparent election in more than a decade, and President Nicolas Maduro
announced in October that the "revolution" would never give up power. If the government-controlled national
electoral council announces a victory for Mr. Maduro's United Socialist Party of Venezuela (PSUV) and its
ally, the Communist Party, it would not be surprising to see an angry electorate take to the streets.
That could produce another bloody crackdown. Last year the government repressed protests across the
country by detaining more than 3,000, mostly students. Many were tortured. The gangland-style slaying of an
opposition activist at a rally last month is a reminder that the government and its Cuban coaches have
fomented deep hatred among supporters to protect its power. Yet rebellion simmers and economic hardship
could cause it to boil over.
Venezuela is home to some of the world's largest petroleum reserves, and for decades it has been heavily
dependent on black gold to earn foreign exchange. As an importer of many foodstuffs as well as auto parts,
electronics and inputs for manufacturing of everything from autos to laundry detergent, it needs dollars to pay
the bills.
When oil prices were north of $80 a barrel Chavez bought the acquiescence of the public -- both the poor and
some members of the entrepreneurial classes -- to consolidate power. He confiscated property, gagged the
media and destroyed dissident business owners. Many of the most productive fled the country. Others like
media tycoon Gustavo Cisneros were allowed to keep their assets providing they agreed to self-censorship.
When managers and workers at the state-owned oil company PdVSA went on strike to protest Chavez's
power grab, he fired them and replaced them with loyalists. The loss of professionals hit output, revenues and
growth.
Now the central bank is running low on international reserves, productive capacity has been crippled, and
the central bank prints local currency on demand from the government, generating hyperinflation. Since Nov.
11, when price controls were imposed on eggs, there are no eggs to be found in formal markets. "That'll cost
them," a local wrote to me last week, talking about the PSUV.
It's improbable that this election was fair. Neither the Chavez government (1999-2013) nor Mr. Maduro has
allowed voter rolls to be independently audited. Mr. Maduro refused an observer mission from the
Organization of American States. Instead he brought in the South American multilateral known as Unasur,
which is controlled by the pro-Cuba governments of Venezuela, Argentina, Bolivia, Ecuador and Brazil.
Opposition volunteers did their best to act as ballot-box watchdogs. But in many precincts it was dangerous to
remain at the voting stations as evening fell because of high-crime and pro-government gangs, and it is likely
many went home out of fear. If so, many government operatives had no oversight when counting votes.
To claim victory the opposition needed to win by a wide margin. Polls were supposed to close at 6 p.m. (5:30
p.m. EST) unless there were still voters in line. Opposition members told me that in the last presidential
election, in April 2013, polls were kept open after 6 p.m. even when there were no lines. They allege that
pro-government activists used that time to identify no-show voters and fill in ballots for them, giving Mr.
Maduro his narrow victory.
The national electoral council has favored the government by gerrymandering congressional districts and
designing the ballots to confuse the voter. Moreover, each of the 24 states also automatically gets two seats,

--- Page 22 ---
Page 22 of 210 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
regardless of population. So a landslide for opposition candidates in a large state has the same effect as a
narrow chavista victory in a sparsely populated state that the government easily controls. Scores of the most
popular opposition politicians spent the election in jail, barred from running.
Even if the regime allows a narrow opposition victory, it's hard to envision Mr. Maduro ceding power. More
likely, a government defeat would be followed by the jailing or expulsion of opposition congressmen as has
been done in the past, or by buying them off. Another card that Mr. Maduro has yet to play is the very Soviet
"law of popular power," which would make local Bolivarian councils the lawmakers and congress irrelevant.
The formalities of how the chavistas will stay aren't yet known. But that they plan to stay you can be sure.

Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- News Challenge: The Month in Funds and Investing: Test Your Smarts on Coke, Gold, Vanguard
Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- News Challenge: The Month in Funds and
Investing: Test Your Smarts on Coke, Gold, Vanguard
By Victor Reklaitis
970 words
7 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
R11
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The stock market in November added a tiny bit to the previous month's big advance, with the Dow Jones
Industrial Average edging up 0.3%. Individual stocks made more-dramatic moves, with some retailers diving
and one drugmaker soaring.
Billionaire investors stayed in the spotlight, while gold and the euro got roughed up. Plus, cuts in the expense
ratios of some exchange-traded funds sparked fresh talk about a fee war.
How much do you know about last month's news in fund investing and the markets? Here's a quiz to test your
knowledge.
---
1. How did the Dow industrials finish on the first day of trading after the Paris terror attacks?
A. down 252 points
B. down 72 points
C. basically flat
D. up 238 points
ANSWER: D. The industrial average closed with a gain of 237.77 points on Nov. 16 as financial markets
broadly overcame worries stemming from the attacks. Energy stocks helped lead the Dow higher as oil prices
rose on expectations of more Mideast violence.
---
2. Fill in the blank: Hedge-fund manager William Ackman said Coca-Cola Co. has "probably done more to
create _____ on a global basis than any other company in the world."
A. jitters and indigestion
B. tooth decay and sugar highs
C. obesity and diabetes
D. alertness and happiness
ANSWER: C. Mr. Ackman said he wouldn't own Coke shares, linking the soft-drink giant to obesity and
diabetes in remarks at a New York conference last month. He took a shot at Warren Buffett's investments in
Coke after Mr. Buffett's longtime sidekick, Charles Munger, criticized Mr. Ackman's bet on Valeant
Pharmaceuticals International Inc. Coke defended its products and called the remarks "irresponsible."
---
3. Shares in tiny KaloBios Pharmaceuticals Inc. surged in mid-November as a controversial figure became a
major shareholder. Who was it?
A. William Ackman
B. Tom Brady

--- Page 24 ---
Page 24 of 210 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
C. Miley Cyrus
D. Martin Shkreli
ANSWER: D. Mr. Shkreli and an investor group disclosed they owned a majority stake. He drew flak in
September as his Turing Pharmaceuticals AG increased the price of an antiparasite tablet more than 50-fold.
---
4. To what level did BlackRock Inc. drop the expense ratio for its iShares Core S&P Total U.S. Stock Market
ETF?
A. 0.01%, or $1 a year for every $10,000 invested
B. 0.03%
C. 0.30%
D. 0.00%, or free
ANSWER: B. Charles Schwab Corp. later matched the cut with its Schwab U.S. Large-Cap ETF, after saying
its "intention has always been to be the price leader." BlackRock's move on Nov. 10 was also seen as a
challenge to Vanguard Group.
---
5. Gold futures in November suffered their biggest monthly percentage loss since when?
A. August 1993
B. August 2008
C. June 2013
D. July 2015
ANSWER: C. Gold futures lost nearly 7% in November, leaving them down about 10% for the year at
month's end. Growing expectations that the Federal Reserve will raise interest rates at its Dec. 15-16
meeting have weighed on gold, which doesn't pay interest.
---
6. Fill in the blank: As Macy's Inc. posted weaker-than-expected quarterly sales, Chief Executive Terry
Lundgren said, "The retail industry is going through _____."
A. hell
B. a tough period
C. some Amazon-driven stress
D. a "come to Jesus" moment
ANSWER: B. Mr. Lundgren summed up the difficulties of department-store chains by saying that although
consumer spending seemed healthy, many people were buying items that department stores don't sell, such
as cars and gadgets.
---
7. China's Shanghai Composite Index fell 5.5% on Nov. 27, its largest daily drop since August. What got
blamed for the selloff?
A. Rumors of a potential big devaluation of the yuan
B. Alibaba Group Holding Ltd. and rivals disclosing soft sales for Singles' Day
C. Beijing signaling it could tolerate slower growth, setting 6.5% as a new floor
D. Beijing stepping up its probe into three major Chinese brokerage firms

--- Page 25 ---
Page 25 of 210 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
ANSWER: D. Citic Securities Co., Guosen Securities Co. and Haitong Securities Co. said they would
cooperate with authorities, who are looking into possible violations of securities rules. Alibaba actually
reported blockbuster sales for Singles' Day.
---
8. Activist investor Carl Icahn revealed a 7.1% stake in which storied company that has struggled to adapt?
A. Xerox Corp.
B. Eastman Kodak Co.
C. Sears Holdings Corp.
D. Studebaker Motor Co.
ANSWER: A. Xerox said it welcomed "constructive dialogue with shareholders."
---
9. Vanguard Group last month signaled it is on pace to break a record it set last year for the largest annual
inflow for a mutual-fund firm. How much investor money did it take in through the first 10 months of 2015?
A. $565 million
B. $105 billion
C. $196 billion
D. $1.2 trillion
ANSWER: C. The index-investing pioneer said it had attracted a net $196 billion through the end of October,
topping the $163 billion it drew in the same period last year. Vanguard took in $216 billion in 2014 to set the
annual record.
---
10. How much had the euro fallen against the dollar this year at month's end?
A. 8.3%
B. 10.5%
C. 12.7%
D. 20.1%
ANSWER: C. The euro in November lost 4% versus the dollar, leaving the shared currency down 12.7% for
the year at month's end against its U.S. counterpart. Analysts attribute the slide to bets that U.S. interest
rates will rise soon, while the eurozone's monetary policy remains looser.

Dow Rises 370 to Cap Rocky Week
Friday's Markets
Dow Rises 370 to Cap Rocky Week
By Leslie Josephs and Lisa Beilfuss
835 words
5 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Dow industrials surged to end a tumultuous week after a strong U.S. jobs report cleared the way for the
Federal Reserve to raise interest rates.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 369.96 points, or 2.1%, to 17847.63, for its biggest increase since
Sept. 8. The S&P 500 rose 42.07, or 2.1%, to 2091.69, while the Nasdaq Composite rose 104.74, or 2.1%, to
5142.27.
The rally more than reversed the previous session's sharp losses, when disappointment over the size of the
European Central Bank's fresh stimulus measures caused investors to unwind many popular bets they had
piled into this year.
However, Friday's surge only narrowly tipped the Dow back into positive territory for 2015, a year that has
experienced large swings. The Dow is up 0.1% so far this year and rose 0.3% this past week.
"I struggle to think of a scenario where the S&P [500] is up double digits, but I can easily think of scenarios
where we're down double digits" next year, said Michael Fredericks, portfolio manager of the BlackRock
Multi-Asset Income fund. "We've been cutting back on our U.S. equity exposure over the last month."
The stock market faces challenges as it heads into 2016, particularly from tepid global growth and troubled
sectors such as energy. The S&P 500's energy-company index lost 0.5% Friday and is down 19% for the
year.
U.S. crude oil declined 2.7% to $39.97 a barrel as the Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries
ended a meeting without an agreement to restrain production, leaving members to continue pumping crude at
near-record levels into an oversupplied market.
Traders said buyers took advantage of Thursday's stock-price declines to buy at cheaper levels, especially
since there is little time for the market to post a strong recovery before the end of the year.
"We'll probably almost run out of time for things to look great by the year-end," said Randy Frederick,
managing director of trading and derivatives at Charles Schwab. It could be possible "if we have three weeks
of gains like today, but that sounds unlikely."
The jobs report, some investors said, removed lingering uncertainty in the market about whether the economy
is healthy enough for the Fed to start raising interest rates at its Dec. 15-16 meeting.
Investors also believe the pace of rate increases would be slow amid an uneven pace of global economic
growth.
The U.S. Labor Department said nonfarm payrolls increased a seasonally adjusted 211,000 jobs in
November, beating expectations for 200,000 jobs.
"This pretty much locks in the first rate hike this month," said Brian Nick, head of tactical asset allocation at
UBS Wealth Management Americas.
All 30 Dow components rose. The biggest gainer, Apple, advanced $3.83, or 3.3%, to $119.03.
The rate-sensitive financial sector gained 2.7% and helped lead Friday's rally. A rate increase should bode
well for financial companies, investors said, because lenders will be able to charge borrowers more while
earning more on reserves.
J.P. Morgan shares rose 2.09, or 3.2%, to 67.89.

The Road to the White House
Bookshelf
The Road to the White House
By Walter Russell Mead
998 words
4 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Triumph of William McKinley
By Karl Rove
(Simon & Schuster, 482 pages, $32.50)
It is more common in Britain than in the U.S. for leading political figures to write about history. That is a pity;
nobody knows the business of politics as intimately as those who work in the field. Karl Rove's book on the
1896 election shows that American pols can write as well and as knowingly about political history as their
British counterparts.
William McKinley's rise to the presidency is one of the major turning points in U.S. history, but it has never
received the attention it deserves. This is partly because many of its consequences were at the congressional
rather than presidential level. Republicans, after all, had dominated the White House since Lincoln's 1860
victory, with only the two nonconsecutive Grover Cleveland victories breaking the GOP lock on the executive
mansion.
But this dominance was weaker than it looked. Once Reconstruction ended in 1877, and white Southern
Democrats reasserted their dominance by intimidating black and white Republican voters, the GOP could no
longer count on controlling Congress. This changed after the 1896 election. Republicans would control the
Senate for 30 of the next 36 years and the House for 26.
To help his readers understand McKinley's accomplishment, Mr. Rove, who writes a weekly column for this
paper, must take on the herculean task of explaining the meaning of the tariff and monetary controversies to
21st-century readers. The two burning issues of the contest between McKinley and William Jennings Bryan
were the gold standard and protectionism. The Republicans favored both; the Democrats hated them. Few
readers will have any idea about these debates, and it is to Mr. Rove's great credit that he brings them to life.
At a time when the federal income tax did not exist, protectionism was both a way to raise money for the
federal government (through high duties on imported goods) and a form of industrial policy. Revenue was
important to Republicans: Pensions for Civil War veterans helped hold the party together in the North. High
tariff walls protected American industries and favored agricultural interests, like wool, from foreign
competition. This led to increased investment in American industrial capacity as foreign and domestic
investors sought to enjoy the benefits of an expanding market. Republicans argued that the resulting high
level of investment fostered competition, ensuring that protected industries remained efficient. At the same
time, job growth increased the demand for labor, and this, McKinley maintained, raised the wages of
American workers.
This last was the feature that helped the GOP compete against the Democrats for the votes of industrial
workers in 1896. McKinley's advocacy made protectionism look like a populist, wage-raising policy, and high
tariffs would remain a cornerstone of American politics until the failure of the Hawley-Smoot tariff during the
Depression.
McKinley expected protectionism to dominate the campaign, and the emergence of the "free silver" question
discomfited him. In the late 19th century, advocates of what today would be called loose monetary policy
argued that, in addition to minting gold dollars, the U.S. should mint large quantities of silver currency, at a
price ratio of 16 ounces of silver equal to one ounce of gold. Because the market value of silver was
significantly below the 16-to-1 ratio, coining silver dollars would, proponents argued, expand the money
supply, stimulate more economic activity and give debtors some relief.
The Depression of 1893 had brought widespread unemployment; Democratic President Grover Cleveland
refused to heed the clamor for silver coinage; the Depression deepened, and pro-silver insurgents took

--- Page 29 ---
Page 29 of 210 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
control of the Democratic Party machinery during the primary campaign. Bryan's famous "Cross of Gold"
speech at the Democratic convention in the summer of 1896 led to his nomination.
Faced with an opponent for whom bimetallism was the most important issue, McKinley stumbled initially, but
his campaign quickly developed powerful counterarguments. "Debasing" the currency, McKinley said, wasn't
just dishonest; it would cheat farmers and others who would be paid for their work in cheaper dollars even as
inflation eroded their living standards. In contrast to historians like Howard Zinn, who portray Bryan's
free-silver crusade as a great populist uprising, Mr. Rove offers a portrait of McKinley's GOP coalition as a
pro-worker movement whose success depended on its ability to improve the lives of ordinary Americans.
Mr. Rove's narration of the fall campaign is lively and compelling. Bryan stormed the country by train;
McKinley conducted a classic "front porch" campaign, making speeches to delegations ranging from
high-school bands to ex-Confederate veterans in a carefully orchestrated display. Mr. Rove's own campaign
experience allows him to analyze both strategies.
McKinley's victory ensured the triumph of industry over agriculture and the future over the past. While Bryan
held the South and made gains in the high plains and the mountain states, McKinley carried the Republican
heartland in New England, adding swing states like New York and Pennsylvania and managing to pry
Kentucky and Maryland from the Democrats. With 271 electoral votes compared with Bryan's 176 and an
outright majority of the popular vote, McKinley had the strongest mandate of any president since Grant.
Although Mr. Rove has written a very good history of the 1896 campaign, he is ultimately less interested in
the 19th century than he is in the 21st. As he analyzes McKinley's approach to coalition building and crafting
a sympathetic image that could reach beyond traditional GOP voters, he seems to be advising current
political figures as much as analyzing the past. The result is a rigorous book that should be studied by anyone
interested in following McKinley to the White House.
---
Mr. Mead is a professor at Bard College and a distinguished scholar in American strategy and statesmanship
at the Hudson Institute. Follow him on Twitter@wrmead.

Health-care companies' shares led the market higher. The S&P 500's index for the sector gained 1.7%

UnitedHealth Group rose $3.55, or 3.2%, to $116.26. Merck added 1.56, or 2.9%, to 54.57, while Pfizer

The S&P 500's indexes of materials and industrial stocks posted more modest gains behind the broader
market, gaining 0.7% and 0.6%, respectively, after a key gauge of U.S. manufacturing showed that activity in
the sector unexpectedly fell into contraction territory for the first time in three years.
The Institute for Supply Management's index declined to 48.6 in November from 50.1 in October, its lowest
level since 2009. Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal expected the index to edge up to 50.5.
The weak manufacturing activity could mean that the Federal Reserve will be cautious if or when it raises
interest rates, said Tom Porcelli, chief U.S. economist at RBC Capital Markets.
Investors are looking for clues from U.S. employment data and from Fed Chairwoman Janet Yellen this week
on whether the Fed will go ahead with an expected increase to interest rates later this month.
"December is a done deal," Mr. Porcelli said, but added that "this [manufacturing] number reinforces the case
that [further increases] could be a slow pace."
Ms. Yellen is scheduled to speak at the Economic Club of Washington on Wednesday and to U.S. lawmakers
Thursday.
"She's going to guide the market," said Quincy Krosby, market strategist at Prudential Financial. "She doesn't
want the market to be surprised."
While the Fed is expected to raise interest rates this month for the first time in nearly a decade, Europe's
central bank is expected to further easy monetary policy this week.
"The fact they'll be taking different routes -- the ECB loosening, the Fed tightening -- is a given; what markets
need now is guidance on how fast they'll be traveling and how far they'll end up going," said Neil Williams,
group chief economist at Hermes Investment Management.
The Stoxx Europe 600 fell 0.3% Tuesday after notching a 2.7% gain for November.
Banking shares gained in Europe after the Bank of England said it would ease pressure on U.K. banks to
hold more capital. Lloyds Banking Group climbed two pence, or 2.4%, to 75 pence ($1.13) in London, while
Barclays added 10 pence, or 4.6%, to GBP 2.34 and Royal Bank of Scotland Group rose 10 pence, or 3.2%,
to GBP 3.12.

Target lost 94 cents, or 1.3%, to 72.50 after the retailer reported its website had technical problems Monday

Monday's losses conclude what was a ho-hum month for stocks in November, marking a breather for
investors following wild swings in the past few months. Stocks tumbled in August and September, only to
recover sharply in October. In the past two months, the Dow has surged 8.8%, while the S&P is up 8.4%.
Later this week, investors will turn their focus to the November jobs report, scheduled for release Friday. The
report is expected to show that U.S. employers added 205,000 jobs in November, while the unemployment
rate is expected to remain unchanged at 5%, according to economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal.

December.
Anticipation of a rate rise also has driven up the U.S. dollar. The euro fell 0.3% against the dollar Monday to

British pound dipped below $1.50 intraday Monday for the first time since April 23 and closed at $1.5056.
The Fed's expected decision to tighten monetary policy stands in contrast to central-bank policies
elsewhere. The European Central Bank, for example, is expected to cut interest rates deeper into negative
territory this week as it battles to drive up ultralow inflation.

in my gas tank, I can put it under the tree," said Lamar Villere, portfolio manager at Villere & Co.

in commodities that dragged down the broader markets, particularly energy companies.

World News: Soccer Club Launched Argentine Presidential Favorite
World News: Soccer Club Launched Argentine Presidential Favorite
By John Otis
699 words
21 November 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
A10
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BUENOS AIRES -- Mauricio Macri, who is favored in the polls to unseat Argentina's ruling party in Sunday's
presidential election, forged his political chops by running something dear to the hearts of Argentines: a
soccer club.
As elected president of the legendary Boca Juniors from 1995 to 2007, Mr. Macri turned an organization that
was losing matches and money into an international brand name, winning more titles than in any other
12-year stretch in team history. Mr. Macri parlayed that run into two terms as the business-friendly mayor of
Buenos Aires, positioning himself for his presidential run against Daniel Scioli, the governor of Buenos Aires
province.
The Boca job was a pressure cooker. The club has such a devoted following that some fans' dying wish is to
have their ashes sprinkled on the pitch of "the chocolate box," its cramped and creaky 1940s-era stadium.
But Mr. Macri, the son of one of the country's wealthiest businessmen, says he found there his template for
success at Boca.
"I applied a lot of the things I learned at Boca in the city and I'm going to continue applying them in the future,"
he said.
It often seems as if there is no daylight between Argentine politics and soccer. Retired players sometimes run
for office and government officials use the sport for their own ends. Like the NFL's Green Bay Packers,
Argentina's soccer clubs are nonprofits with thousands of citizen shareholders. Every four years, they vote for
president among candidates who spend huge sums stumping for support.
Mr. Macri, 56 years old, campaigned for the Boca presidency two decades ago after working for the
construction and real-estate development firms founded by his father, the industrialist Franco Macri. The two
men had often clashed, and running the soccer club helped Mr. Macri forge his own identity.
"It was a torturous relationship. Boca gave him oxygen and autonomy," said Martin D'Alessandro, president of
the Argentine Association of Political Analysis.
The job also helped Mr. Macri's soften his blue-blood image by connecting with the team's bedrock fans in the
capital's rough waterfront neighborhood of La Boca, where the club is based.
Still, Mr. Scioli, the standard-bearer of the populist Peronist movement, says Mr. Macri is an out-of-touch
aristocrat who would dismantle the social-welfare programs creating by departing President Cristina Kirchner
in favor of a more laissez-faire approach. Mr. Macri has said the role of Argentina's president should like that
of a stadium groundskeeper, who mows the grass and marks the boundary lines but then moves off the field
to let the teams play.
Forging a culture of fiscal responsibility -- a main theme of his presidential bid -- was Mr. Macri's first
challenge at the club. When he arrived there, the club's debts were being covered by selling off real estate
and star players.
During his first two years, he promoted the team's promising minor leaguers, leaving the team to flounder on
the field. But by 1998, Boca began to click and embarked on a 40-game unbeaten streak. All told it won 16
national and international titles under Mr. Macri.
As the team's popularity exploded, Mr. Marci raised ticket prices, added corporate box seats and turned the
team into a merchandising juggernaut. Gift shops sold everything from the blue-and-gold Boca jerseys to
Boca cologne. Mr. Macri opened a team museum and spread the Boca name overseas through soccer clinics
and tournaments.

--- Page 43 ---
Page 43 of 210 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Other Argentine clubs adopted Mr. Macri's professional management style, and scholars at Harvard Business
School started researching the Boca business model.
Critics say Mr. Macri did little, however, to confront the violent hooligans that follow the team, and say that by
taking Boca upscale, he turned his back on the club's more humble fan base.
As president, Mr. Macri would take over a country with a stagnant economy, 25% annual inflation, and
fast-dwindling central-bank reserves. Turning around Boca took several years; as Argentina's leader, Mr.
Macri would be called upon to move faster.

REVIEW --- Holiday Books: Inflationist From the Start
REVIEW --- Holiday Books: Inflationist From the Start
By Charles R. Morris
995 words
21 November 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C10
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Money Makers
By Eric Rauchway
Basic, 305 pages, $28.99
Franklin Roosevelt was sailing back to Washington on the USS Indianapolis in the summer of 1933 when he
got a cable from his secretary of state, Cordell Hull. It was his fourth month in office. Hull was attending an
international monetary conference in London and reported that it was nearing an agreement "that gold should
be reestablished as the international measure of exchange value."
The small American team in London, including Hull, did not know the mind of their president. After a couple of
days of pondering, Roosevelt wrote a return message stating that gold was "among the old fetishes of
so-called international bankers" and that a premature fixing of a gold valuation would be "a catastrophe
amounting to a world tragedy." In a newspaper column two days later John Maynard Keynes, who was
becoming widely recognized as an important monetary theorist, proclaimed that Roosevelt was "magnificently
right."
Roosevelt's decision to float the dollar was one of the most portentous policy decisions in the long struggle to
escape the dark hole of the Great Depression. In "The Money Makers," Eric Rauchway -- a historian at the
University of California, Davis -- presents a close-to-the-ground account of the political and intellectual forces
that drove the decision. Mr. Rauchway's prose is lucid and unpretentious, and his explanations of technical
monetary issues are smoothly integrated with the flow of the story. Roughly the first half of the book is
devoted to Roosevelt's devaluation decision, while the second half takes us through the war years to the
Bretton Woods conference that for a full generation enshrined the U.S. dollar as the world's de facto gold
standard.
All the belligerents in World War I issued truckloads of fiat money, and all but the United States were forced
off the gold standard. The U.S. brought its inflation under quasi-control by 1921 but at a level of domestic
prices still considerably higher than implied by the official gold-dollar parity of $20.67. Orthodox financial
opinion prescribed yet more deflation, although it was wreaking havoc in the agricultural heartland. Deflation
drove down crop prices even as farm mortgages and other debt remained fixed at their old values. Farmers
defaulted wholesale, triggering cascading waves of bank failures. When FDR was finally inaugurated on
March 4, 1933, most banks were shuttered throughout the country. His first official act was to declare a
nationwide bank holiday to sort out the mess.
Mr. Rauchway makes a good case that Roosevelt had a firm grasp of the monetary realities and was an
inflationist from the start. It was also good politics: Roosevelt had massive congressional majorities, heavily
tilted toward the always-inflationist farm belt. But the notion of cheapening the dollar split the administration.
The chairman of the Federal Reserve, Eugene Black, and the undersecretary of the Treasury, Dean
Acheson, resigned over the issue. James Warburg, a prominent banker, a friend of Roosevelt and an
important adviser, openly broke with the president and began organizing economists and bankers to oppose
the new policies.
Henry Morgenthau Jr., the Treasury secretary and an orchardist in his private life, along with George Warren,
a Cornell agricultural professor, spearheaded the inflationist cause. Keynes was not prominently involved in
the decision-making. He and Roosevelt met only once, in a session that seems to have been pleasant but
insubstantial. But Keynes was a shrewd lobbyist and made a point of keeping key Roosevelt advisers well
supplied with inflationist arguments.
Using powers arguably possessed by one of Hoover's financial agencies, the administration began buying
gold like any other commodity. Sensing a coming devaluation, traders rapidly bid up the price. After

--- Page 45 ---
Page 45 of 210 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Congress passed the Gold Reserve Act in early 1934, Roosevelt fixed the dollar-price of gold at $35 per
ounce. Mr. Rauchway writes: "The United States had a dollar with a fixed notional value in gold, just as it had
at the start of March 1933, but almost everything about the money was new. Not only was it worth less than it
had been, but no ordinary citizen could get gold for paper money. The dollar had a value only for settling
international accounts. Moreover, the value of the dollar was fixed only for so long as the president thought it
should stay fixed . . . the president would adjust that peg as he believed economic circumstances required."
The acid test of the new policy, of course, was whether it would help growth, and it passed with flying colors.
Real growth in Roosevelt's first term averaged about 9%, a peacetime record. There was a short, sharp
recession in 1937-38 when the money supply was prematurely tightened, but robust growth resumed after
policies were adjusted. Later research shows that, as a group, the countries that left the gold standard in the
early 1930s enjoyed the most rapid recoveries.
Mr. Rauchway uses the gold decision as the fulcrum of his book. The second half shifts to a 10,000-foot
aerial view, recording the ways in which the invigorated, and soon to be almighty, dollar financed the Allied
powers in World War II. He traces the mutually suspicious dance with the Soviets over Lend-Lease and much
else, including the Bretton Woods negotiations of 1944. Along the way, he offers a fascinating portrait of the
communist spy Harry Dexter White, who was the senior American official at Bretton Woods. While it is
something of a whirl of names and places, especially in contrast to the concentrated intensity of the book's
first half, there is enough to hold the reader's interest, and a unifying theme: the still-contentious proposition
that governments can and should manage their currencies and economies to produce optimum growth.
---
Mr. Morris, a fellow at the Century Foundation, is working on a book on the crash of 1929-33.

U.S. stocks rallied after minutes from last month's Federal Reserve meeting showed most officials anticipated
economic conditions could be strong enough for a December interest-rate increase.
Major indexes posted their biggest one-day gains in nearly a month. The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose
247.66 points, or 1.4%, to 17737.16. The S&P 500 rose 33.14, or 1.6%, to 2083.58, and the Nasdaq

"I think it's a relief for the market that in the opinion of the Fed policy makers the economy is not falling apart,"
said Keith Bliss, senior vice president at brokerage Cuttone & Co.

between what banks charge on loans and what they pay for deposits.

OppenheimerFunds. "The uncertainty is detrimental," he said, adding that the Oppenheimer Equity Income
Fund that he manages has outsize exposure to some banks.
But not all investors are convinced that the Fed will raise rates next month.
Federal-funds futures, used by investors and traders to place bets on central-bank policy, showed
Wednesday that they see a 68% likelihood of a Fed rate increase at its Dec. 15-16 policy meeting, according
to data from CME Group.
The probability was 72% before the minutes were released. It had been 38% before Fed officials on Oct. 28
said they might raise short-term rates in December. Their statement, after a two-day policy meeting, sent the
Dow industrials up 1.1% that day.
The yield on the 10-year Treasury note rose to 2.269%, from 2.262% on Tuesday. Yields rise as prices fall.

Dollar's Revival Poses New Threat
Dollar's Revival Poses New Threat
By James Ramage and Nicole Friedman
707 words
18 November 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The dollar rallied to a seven-month high against the euro and a three-month high against the yen Tuesday,
while copper and oil prices slumped anew, intensifying investor scrutiny of the currency-commodity nexus.
Expectations that the Federal Reserve will raise interest rates next month for the first time since 2006 have
fueled an autumn dollar revival. The U.S. currency is up 5% against the euro and 2% against the yen since
the start of October, when riskier assets such as emerging-market stocks and bonds began staging a strong
recovery from August's global-growth scare.
Commodity prices have slumped over the same period, reflecting deepening concerns about hefty supply and
the appetite for the world's largest consumer of many raw materials, China. Since Oct. 1, a period in which
expectations for expansive central-bank policy in Europe and China have helped support stock and bond
prices, Nymex crude futures are down 9.1%, copper is off 8.7% and gold has dropped 4.1%.
Continued dollar gains will likely add to the pressure on oil and other commodity prices, analysts said, as
globally traded commodities are priced in dollars. Ripples from the dollar's rise were evident in late 2014 and
early this year, when emerging-market currencies and other commodity-related assets were battered.
"As a value-oriented investor, I keep looking at emerging markets and commodities in the belief that there is
value in these assets, but the downside momentum has been concerning," said James Solloway, portfolio
manager at SEI, which oversees $262 billion.
While many investors and traders say the scale of dollar gains and commodity-price declines in the past two
years should limit the size of any market moves between now and year-end, some are keeping a close eye
on whether the dollar appears to be gaining momentum.
"A stronger dollar could make this worse," Mr. Solloway added.
Supporting the dollar-bull camp, growth prospects look brighter in the U.S. than they do in Europe, Japan or
many developing nations. Bullish dollar bets more than doubled to $33.3 billion between mid-October and the
week ending Nov. 10, according to the latest Commodity Futures Trading Commission data.
The latest advance reflects increased confidence among traders and investors that the Federal Reserve will
lift rates more than once in coming months.
Adding to the dollar's attractiveness, the European Central Bank appears to be on the verge of further easing
monetary policy, a move that would likely push down the euro and further bolster the dollar. Investors often
seek out countries where interest rates are rising.
"We think U.S. [economic] data will be on the positive side, relative to expectations," said Paul Lambert, head
of currency at Insight Investment, with $605 billion under management. Insight has bets for the U.S. currency
to strengthen against the yen and euro.
Dollar gains already are hitting crude markets. U.S. oil prices have fallen 13% in November, as domestic
crude stockpiles grew more than expected.
"The dollar and U.S. inventories have been the downward pressure in November," said Dan Pickering, chief
investment officer at Tudor, Pickering, Holt & Co. Asset Management in Houston, which oversees $1.7 billion.
Some investors are skeptical that the dollar has much room to rise because a rate increase in the U.S. has
been so well telegraphed.

--- Page 51 ---
Page 51 of 210 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"History tells us the dollar's trajectory is less clear once the hiking cycle begins," said Scott DiMaggio, director
of global fixed-income investments at AllianceBernstein LP. "It's enough to keep people from taking on large
positions."
Analysts at Morgan Stanley forecast the greenback will rise to parity with the euro from a recent $1.06 by the
end of 2016 and strengthen to 125 yen for the first time since August from a recent 123. Goldman Sachs
Group Inc. analysts are more bullish, calling for one euro to buy 85 U.S. cents and one dollar to buy 135 yen.
On Tuesday, the dollar advanced 0.4% against the euro, as the common currency bought $1.0644. The
greenback rose 0.2% versus the yen to 123.44 yen.
